Team","enclosureType":"audio/mpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://dts.podtrac.com/redirect.mp3/traffic.libsyn.com/secure/productivityshow/313_-_The_Productivity_Show_-_The_Best_Apps_and_Tools_To_Work_From_Home_Productively.mp3","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/TheProductivityShow_FB-e1482589435887.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn85@2020_2/2020/08/25/01-05-11-115_684a6465226bdb67.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/itunes-logo.150.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn72@2020_4/2020/08/25/01-05-49-191_3de616686d8dfb4b.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/sticher.150.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn23@2020_2/2020/08/25/01-05-50-320_80c57ad218498494.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/overcast_final1.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn23@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-05-49-489_c087859209696cac.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/pocket-cast.150.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn100@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-05-48-325_323e6c883aefdcda.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317508237},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","title":"The Most Important Productivity Strategy For WFH People During The Pandemic","link":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/?p=16235","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone wp-image-15953 size-medium_large\" src=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps-768x513.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"513\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps-768x513.jpg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps-500x334.jpg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps-640x428.jpg 640w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps.jpg 1000w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 768px) 100vw, 768px\" /></p><p><em>Photo credit: <a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/alinasternberg/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Alina Sternberg</a></em></p><p>Working from home comes with its own challenges and it’s a relatively new concept for the majority of people. When you have children at home, no co-workers, and a non-existent daily schedule it seems like it requires a lot of discipline, effort, and focus to get work done at home.</p><p>There’s some truth to that. When I first started in 2009, I had no clue what to do and <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/organization/asian-efficiency-guide-working-productively-home/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">how to be productive at home</a>. Through hundreds of experiments, I figured out how to be productive while working from home. This birthed Asian Efficiency where I was inspired to blog every week to help family and friends. This led to the creation of one of the most popular productivity blogs, <a href=\"http://theproductivityshow.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">The Productivity Show</a> which is the #1 podcast on productivity with close to 9 million downloads, and <a href=\"https://asianefficiencygo.com/focus-filter-evergreen/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">dozens of courses</a> at the company.</p><p>Our goal at AE is to help people become more productive at work and in life. Now that we’re a few months in a pandemic, there’s one productivity strategy I’ve discovered that is powerful yet no one is talking about this. This has me very concerned because it’s going to lead to major troubles down the line.</p><h2>My Major Concern For Everyone Who’s Working Right Now</h2><p>Work and life seem to blend in and feel the same now. Everyone is working from home and there’s no clear separation between work and your personal life. Add in that we’re stuck, cannot go anywhere and there’s no clear end in sight for the pandemic, <strong>I forecast major burnout for many people in the next 6-12 months.</strong></p><p>This is what has me very concerned for everyone who’s fortunate enough to have a job and is working to pay their bills. While that is a blessing, I would caution you to pay attention to signs of burnout. <a href=\"https://www.psychologytoday.com/us/blog/high-octane-women/201311/the-tell-tale-signs-burnout-do-you-have-them\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">These include</a>:</p><ul><li><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/3-rituals-that-give-you-more-energy-and-double-your-productivity/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Physical and emotional exhaustion</a> &#8211; a lot of times</li><li>Detachment from work (lack of enthusiasm to show up for work)</li><li>Feeling ineffective, <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/15-ways-get-motivated-dont-feel-like-anything/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">not motivated anymore</a></li><li>You’re sick more often than normal</li><li><a href=\"https://asianefficiencygo.com/serene-sleep-evergreen/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">You’re not sleeping much</a></li><li>Loss of appetite on a regular basis</li><li>Your productivity takes a major dip for a period of time</li></ul><p>Generally, you don’t have a positive outlook on work like you used to. I don’t want you to get to this stage because it can take months to recover from this (it took me 3+ months).</p><p>Now that most of you are new to working from home, you have to be very aware of this. Especially during the crazy times we’re in right now because you’re very susceptible to burnout. In a report from the <a href=\"http://www.ilo.org/wcmsp5/groups/public/---dgreports/---dcomm/---publ/documents/publication/wcms_544138.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">United Nations International Labour Organization</a>, they&#8217;ve found that remote workers are working more hours and sometimes deal with more stress than their office co-workers do. As the owner of Asian Efficiency, <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/podcast/153-remote-team/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a fully remote company with people all over the world</a>, I&#8217;ve seen this to be true too for everyone that works here.</p><h2>The Counterintuitive Insight on Unlimited Vacation Days</h2><p>We have an unlimited vacation day policy at Asian Efficiency. People can take days off whenever they want to and there is no limit to this. I remember when I first introduced this in 2012, I was concerned that people would be taking 3-4 months off a year and that it would be unsustainable to run a company.</p><p>After the first year, we calculated the number of days each individual took off and averaged it out. The results shocked me.</p><p>Asian Efficiency team members, who were educated on productivity strategies and <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/downtime-and-breaks/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">the importance of downtime</a>, we&#8217;re still working more days than the average office worker in the US (which is notorious for only have 15 days off a year including sick days). The average came out to 13 days a year. Compare that to the average European country (anywhere between 25-33 days a year) and I quickly realized something wasn’t adding up.</p><p>It made no sense in my head. They had the option to take as much time off as they wanted and yet they still didn&#8217;t do it. It came to the point where I was forcing people to take time off. In on our 1-on-1 meetings and team meetings, I was telling individuals to leave work and enjoy some days off. If they showed up online on Slack or responded to comments on tasks, I would remind them to get off work. It was a real problem!</p><p>I&#8217;ve talked to other business owners who have similar vacation policies and they&#8217;ve found the same to be true. Remote workers work more than their office counterparts.</p><p>Over the years, our team members have gotten better at taking time off now. Last year, the average number of days off was 36. On our monthly meetings we check-in with everyone’s total days off (it’s on a spreadsheet for everyone to see) and if someone is not taking time off, we all sort of joke around and encourage that person to take some days off. Think of it as positive peer pressure. As a result, we’ve never had any burnout from anyone ever in our company history.</p><h2>What You Need To Do Now</h2><p>Now that you’re working from home during this pandemic, you might have found to be true too that you’re working more and taking fewer days off. You&#8217;re working longer hours, staying up later and you feel like work is more intense than ever before. I do believe that the pandemic has made work a lot more challenging &#8211; even for WFH veterans like myself.</p><p><strong>And this is where I believe one of the best strategies to stay productive during this time is to take time off</strong>. I&#8217;m forcing people at Asian Efficiency to take time off even if they cannot go anywhere. Every week on our team meetings, I’m reminding people to take days off so they don’t get burned out.</p><p>With eight years of experience and insights, I know that remote workers in our company aren&#8217;t going to take time off unless they have a special event to go to such as a wedding, anniversary, family get-togethers, and such. One might say it’s a great testament that people love what they do and it doesn’t feel like work. Part of me is very happy about that and at the same time, I’m also very aware <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/taking-frequent-breaks-key-productivity/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">how important downtime is</a>. More importantly, how much burn out sucks. You feel unmotivated, your health takes a toll, and it can take months to recover from it.</p><p>With the world in a crisis right now and travel restrictions imposed, most of us cannot go anywhere and so we use work as an escape. This could easily lead to burnout and being stressed out. When we have the confluence of a pandemic, uncertainty of the future, financial concerns, working 12-16 hour days, conflicting news reports, and just everyday life&#8230;it can be tough on the mind and body.</p><p>That&#8217;s why I&#8217;m telling everyone at Asian Efficiency to take time off. I care about them, their well-being, and their families. Even if you cannot go anywhere, get your mind off work. You’ll come back refreshed and we’re in it for the long game.</p><p>It’s why we dedicated a whole module to this in our course <strong>Productive At Home</strong> that shows you how to be productive working from home. This course is currently closed for enrollment but will open up soon. Subscribe to our newsletter here to get notified when it opens up again:</p><div class=\"post-optin-wrapper\"><div class=\"inline-fields short-fields feature optin-form text-center post-optin\"><h2>Join Asian Efficiency</h2><p>Get more interesting articles from us:</p><form class=\"km-optin\" data-location=\"post\" data-offer=\"\" accept-charset=\"UTF-8\" action=\"https://forms.ontraport.com/v2.4/form_processor.php\" method=\"post\"><fieldset> <input class=\"text-input\" type=\"text\" name=\"firstname\" value=\"\" placeholder=\"Your First Name\" /> <input class=\"text-input\" type=\"email\" name=\"email\" value=\"\" placeholder=\"Your Email Address\" /> <input class=\"submit-button optin-submit\" type=\"submit\" name=\"submit\" value=\"Send it to me\" /></fieldset> <input type=\"hidden\" name=\"uid\" value=\"p2c7816f29\" /></form></div></div><p>While waiting, <a href=\"https://asianefficiencygo.com/pah-webinar-registration/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">register here</a> to join our FREE training on how to be productive working from home. The training will include apps, tools, and routines that you can use.</p><p>As the saying goes, prevention is better than a cure. It’s better to stop something bad from happening than it is to deal with it after it happened. I’ve had to learn this lesson the hard way multiple times throughout my life and <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/learning-relax-youre-driven-person/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">I hope I can pass on that wisdom to everyone else here today</a>.</p><p>I&#8217;ve taken it a step further by introducing a 4-day workweek for the whole month of July. It&#8217;s the first time we&#8217;re doing this at Asian Efficiency and we&#8217;re all excited to see how it goes. It&#8217;s also my way of introducing a systemic approach for everyone to take time off on top of other days they have scheduled off because I know remote workers tend to not take days off.</p><p>Leading up to our 4-day workweek in July, I&#8217;ve been taking a 6-week 4-day work week and I&#8217;ve found myself more rejuvenated and excited than ever before. During these crazy times, I needed this more than I thought I would.</p><p>So I encourage you to take days off even if you have nowhere to go. There&#8217;s plenty of things you could be doing:</p><ul><li><a href=\"https://asianefficiencygo.com/serene-sleep-evergreen/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Sleeping in and taking a real nap for the first time in forever</a></li><li>Run errands while everyone else is not around</li><li>Get back into reading books and magazines</li><li>Watch Netflix and TV all day without any guilt</li><li>Workout again</li><li><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/organization/20-efficient-home-care-tips/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Pick up the self-care routine that you’ve missed</a></li></ul><p>Pick one thing to do on your day off and you&#8217;ll come back excited for work and have a new sense of excitement for life. If you like more information on how we schedule downtime on a daily/weekly/monthly/quarterly/annual basis, <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/downtime-and-breaks/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">check out this post</a>.</p> ","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 07 Jul 2020 11:00:26 +0000","feedId":10782,"bgimg":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps-768x513.jpg","linkMd5":"5cec379a994ee0266774a4727d48c1cb","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn21@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-05-10-617_7309473aeb4382e9.webp","destWidth":768,"destHeight":513,"sourceBytes":82282,"destBytes":50086,"author":"Thanh Pham","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/03/thanh-sitting-steps-768x513.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn21@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-05-10-617_7309473aeb4382e9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317508238},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","title":"Work From Home Challenge Day 4: Reenergize With Breaks","link":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/?p=16038","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone wp-image-16045 size-full\" src=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/04/happy_-1.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"5760\" height=\"3840\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/04/happy_-1.jpg 5760w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/04/happy_-1-500x333.jpg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/04/happy_-1-768x512.jpg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/04/happy_-1-640x427.jpg 640w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 5760px) 100vw, 5760px\" /></p><p>Welcome to Day 4 of the 5-day Work From Home Challenge. We&#8217;re nearly at the end of the challenge but if you just started today, no worries! You can still do the previous challenges:</p><p><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/work-from-home-day-1-dress-for-work/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Day 1 &#8211; Dress For Work</a><br /> <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/work-from-home-day-2-daily-reset/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Day 2 &#8211; Your Daily Reset</a><br /> <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/work-from-home-day-3-create-time-and-space-to-focus/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Day 3 &#8211; Create Time And Space To Focus</a></p><p>&#8230; and now that you&#8217;re done with the previous three, it&#8217;s time to make sure you don&#8217;t get burnt out working from home.</p><h2>The Burn-Out</h2><p>&#8220;You&#8217;re so lucky you work from home! You have a lot of time to work since you don&#8217;t have to prepare and commute.&#8221;</p><p>That&#8217;s not true.</p><p>People think that just because you work from home, you have all the time in the world since you&#8217;re just at home. You can work anytime. That may be partially true but they forgot to mention that you can still get burnt out when you are working from home because you&#8217;re still working, thinking and actively doing things.</p><p>You still have to prepare for work and do a daily reset just like what we talked about during the <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/work-from-home-day-1-dress-for-work/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">1st</a> and <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/work-from-home-day-2-daily-reset/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">2nd</a> day of our challenge.</p><p>Whether you are single or in a relationship, you also have other responsibilities outside of work. These responsibilities do not stop when you are working from home. We&#8217;re not (just) talking about picking up the groceries and paying bills. You have a responsibility to take care of yourself too (self-care).</p><p>At Asian Efficiency we teach the <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/tea-framework/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">TEA Framework</a>. When you master Time, Energy, and Attention you will be Asian Efficient. When you work from home, you have more time than those who have to commute. What about your energy and attention? After working from home for many years, I’ve discovered that <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/increase-energy-productivity-breakthrough/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">managing your energy</a> is even more important when you’re at home.</p><h2>It&#8217;s All About Energy</h2><p>When you have the time but not the energy to get things done, you are exhausted. Whether you work in an office or at home, that doesn’t differ. I remember Thanh&#8217;s story. One Sunday, he told himself that he will do his taxes. He blocked his entire Sunday just to do his taxes &#8211; he had all the time to it. But he didn&#8217;t end up getting it done. He lounged around and stayed in bed for a long time and just spent the entire day doing other things except for his taxes. When he was journaling that night, he realized that it wasn&#8217;t time that he lacked. He had a whole day for it. What he lacked was energy to do it.</p><p>It&#8217;s the same thing when you procrastinate. Even when you have made the time and have planned out on what you want to do, when you lack energy, you will procrastinate.</p><p>You deplete your energy in several ways:</p><ul><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">lack of sleep or not getting quality sleep</span></li><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">the food you eat</span></li><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">inactivity (sedentary lifestyle)</span></li><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">dehydration</span></li><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">stress</span></li></ul><p>A lot of the things mentioned above, YOU HAVE CONTROL over. But there is something else that you can do to ensure that you always have the energy to finish your tasks every single day.</p><p>Take a break.</p><p>Taking breaks is important for those working from home. Otherwise, you risk of burning yourself out and getting cabin fever. It’s what people describe as “going crazy” when they first start working from home. They’re not taking breaks!</p><p>Several studies prove just how important breaks are. According to a study by <a href=\"https://cdntorkprod.blob.core.windows.net/docs-c5/763/185763/original/tork-takes-back-survey.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Tork</a>, 90% claim that they feel more refreshed and ready to work after their lunch break. Another study of <a href=\"https://www.pnas.org/content/108/17/6889\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">PNAS</a> states that breaks can help prevent decision fatigue especially for roles where you need to make frequent decisions. <a href=\"https://www.psychologytoday.com/us/blog/minding-the-body/201603/become-better-writer-be-frequent-walker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Psychology Today&#8217;s</a> research says that breaks increase creativity AND productivity.</p><p>Imagine as if you&#8217;re running. Can you run for 8 hours straight at the same pace as when you started and not feel tired at all?</p><p>When you drive a car, you can&#8217;t go on forever either. You would need to stop at one point to fill up your tank.</p><p>You are no different. You use your brain a lot when you are working. It will get tired and if you do not let it recover, you will be exhausted and feel burnt out.</p><h2>When And How To Take A Break</h2><p>I used to be very bad at taking breaks. I had to install a <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/motivation/pomodoro-technique-ultimate-guide/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Pomodoro timer</a> on my computer so that it will remind me to take a break. These days, I no longer need it, but if you struggle to remember to take breaks, it is a great solution.</p><p>Now that you know when to take a break, here’s how to use that break wisely. Scrolling through your Twitter feed is not a good example. You might end up feeling riled up because of something you&#8217;ve read. Facebook is another no-no. Stay clear from social media.</p><p>Instead, <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/how-to-get-more-energy/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">do some energizing activities</a>. Stretch your muscles. Go for a short walk. Do some breathing exercises. Do 10 burpees or even jumping jacks. Sitting for several hours on end is bad for your heart&#8217;s health and may even lead to depression. When you move (like stretching), you help supply oxygen to your whole body.</p><p>My workspace is on the upper level of our house. When I need to move around, I go downstairs and pet all our dogs and force my love on our cat.</p><p>Exercise:</p><ul><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Take a look at your calendar for tomorrow</span></li><li style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Put two 30-minute breaks (lunch is excluded) </span></li></ul><p>Let us know in the comments once you are done and what are some of the things you will do during your breaks.</p><div class=\"post-optin-wrapper\"><div class=\"inline-fields short-fields feature optin-form text-center 09:05:08","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","title":"3 Ways To Embrace Change And Stay Productive","link":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/?p=16157","description":"<div style=\"width: 2500px\" class=\"thrv_wrapper tve_image_caption\"> <span class=\"tve_image_frame\"> <img class=\"tve_image\" alt=\"\" src=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/pivot-to-success-portada.jpg\" style=\"width: 2500px\" width=\"2500\" height=\"1875\" scale=\"0\"> </span></div><p>The world is unpredictable. You never know what is going to happen on a given day. Sometimes what has always worked stops working. When something happens that changes the game, it can cause fear, doubt, and worry. Change can be difficult, but there is no formula for success that does not include change. Instead of being afraid, a healthy way to see change is to view it as a pivot towards success.</p><h3>What is a pivot?</h3><p>In her book, <em><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/likes/pivot-jenny-blake/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Pivot: The Only Move That Matters Is Your Next One</a>, </em>author Jenny Blake defines a pivot as &#8220;to switch quickly and successfully from one trajectory to the next.&#8221; Pivot towards success means one is willing to face the fear of change and discover the opportunities that each new day brings.</p><h3>Case study: Restaurant owner Nick Kokonas</h3><p>One of the industries most impacted by the recent worldwide pandemic is food and hospitality. Owners of thriving restaurants were forced to close their doors. Restaurants went from operating at near capacity before the coronavirus to zero customers.</p><p>A case in point is the innovative leadership of entrepreneur Nick Kokonas. Nick is the founder of the phenomenally successful <a href=\"https://www.alinearestaurant.com\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Alinea</a> restaurant in Chicago. Alinea is world-renowned and has been awarded almost every accolade possible in the industry. Nick also built the best in class Tock application, a reservation management platform for restaurants.</p><p>According to an article published by <a href=\"https://www.fastcompany.com/90490819/restaurateur-nick-kokonas-isnt-waiting-for-congress-to-come-to-his-rescue\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Fast Company</a>, he converted his restaurants in the Alinea group from elite dine-in experiences to high-quality carry-out services.</p><p>First, he adjusted the menu. A typical customer at Alinea was paying an average of $375 per person. The chefs found a creative way to prepare a carry-out meal that met their standard of excellence but could be sold at a price point for less than $40 per person. It was a savings of 90% for their customers.</p><p>Second, he leveraged the technology of the Tock application to facilitate carry-out orders instead of table reservations. Instead of having a crisis-reaction, Nick and his team approached the problem with creativity and innovation. Nick said, &#8220;It&#8217;s about making a bridge to the future and keeping our businesses viable so that they can reopen.&#8221;</p><h4>What does that mean for me?</h4><p>Nick is a restaurant owner that wanted to continue selling food. He had to find a new way to be successful because of changes that were beyond his control. You can do the same thing. If you embrace change, you can find new ways to be productive and successful.</p><p>Although no two situations are identical, there are basic elements that make up a successful pivot.</p><h2>Here are three simple ways to embrace change and stay productive</h2><h3>1. Ask yourself clarifying questions</h3><p>Not long ago, I left a company where I worked for more than twenty years. Everything normal for me was changing. I needed a clear direction for my future. As a productive person, I wanted to do something, but I knew that I needed to clarify my thoughts.</p><p>I took a simple inventory of my life. I asked myself <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/how-to-embrace-failure-and-bounce-back-strong/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">what makes me happy</a>. Next, I thought about my skills, my education, and my work experience. Finally, I realized that writing is my greatest passion. My love for writing is an unchanging constant in my life. It has always been the most meaningful part of my work. Once it was clear in my mind, I had the confidence to pursue a career as a writer.</p><h4>Here are some questions you can ask yourself to clarify your thinking</h4><p>1. Learn from the past</p><ul><li>What past accomplishments make you most proud?</li><li>What has my education and experience prepared me to do?</li><li>Name something that if you stopped doing it, you would not miss it.</li></ul><p>2. Observe the present</p><ul><li>What makes me happy?</li><li>List your top skills. What am I good at?</li><li>What is the most fulfilling part of my work?</li></ul><p>3. Dream for the future</p><ul><li>What does change make possible for me?</li><li>Where do I want to be in one year?</li><li>What is my ideal position?</li></ul><p>For more great insights on clarifying questions, read this article on <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/mindsets/5-questions-to-help-you-discover-your-purpose/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">5 Questions to Help You Discover Your Purpose</a>.</p><h3>2. Identify opportunities</h3><p>I have a friend who owns a small café. It is a one-person operation, but no less a successful business than a large corporation like Alinea. My friend closed the doors to her cafe, like all other restaurants. The COVID-19 crisis forced her to make changes.</p><p><img class=\"alignnone size-medium wp-image-16193\" src=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Johanna-Cestas-375x500.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"375\" height=\"500\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Johanna-Cestas-375x500.jpeg 375w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Johanna-Cestas-768x1024.jpeg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Johanna-Cestas-480x640.jpeg 480w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 375px) 100vw, 375px\" /></p><p>After some time to clarify her thoughts, she realized there was an opportunity to do something new. It was near Mother&#8217;s Day. She realized people want to give gifts to their mothers, but normal shopping was not possible because most of the retail stores were closed too. She used her resources to design some gift-baskets that could be delivered to a customer&#8217;s home. Not only was it a great success, but it was the genesis for a new business venture that will continue even when the cafe returns to regular operation.</p><p>A few weeks later, when businesses opened again, I was in a coffee shop talking with one of the employees while I waited on my order. I mentioned the new business selling gift baskets, and it immediately sparked a positive reaction. It was clear that there was an interest in this type of personalized delivery service in the city where I live. When the doors to the restaurant closed, the door of opportunity opened to sell gift baskets.</p><h4>The discovery process made simple</h4><p>The opportunities to pivot may look different in your city or within your company, but there is something new waiting for you to discover it. It may even be an idea you already have in your mind. Now is the time to make a move towards it.</p><p>At first, it may seem overwhelming. We know how you feel, and we can help you <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/4-steps-to-escaping-overwhelm/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">deal with the feeling of being overwhelmed</a>. Making important decisions about your life, your education, or your career can be intimidating, but there is no need to be afraid. I promise you can do this. Don&#8217;t think about it as a mountain that is too high to climb. Instead, see it as a process with some simple steps. Each step takes you closer to your goal. When you arrive, you will feel so much relief. It will give you the confidence to move forward.</p><h4>Here are some practical ways you can identify opportunities without being overwhelmed.</h4><p><strong>1. Idea generation</strong></p><p>Make a list of all of those ideas you have had in the past about how things could improve or something you always wished you could do. You will be surprised how many great ideas are just waiting to surface when you make time for discovery. Many people are visual thinkers, and making a mind map can be a very effective way of collecting and organizing your ideas. For a better understanding of how effective mind-mapping can be for idea generation, read this great article on <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/technology/brainstorming-mind-maps/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Effective Brainstorming Using Mind Maps</a>.</p><p><strong>2. Alignment</strong></p><p>A great place to start is by recognizing how your knowledge and skills may be perfectly aligned with a new opportunity.</p><p><strong>3. Network</strong></p><p>Sit down with a trusted friend and brainstorm ways to innovate in your city or company. Saying things aloud is another excellent way to activate your creativity. Some of the best ideas are born in this kind of spontaneous moments. Creative thinking does not have to be hard. In fact, we have a great resource to help you <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/how-to-identify-and-overcome-the-mental-blocks-that-slow-your-creativity/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">unlock your creativity</a>.</p><h3>3. Define your next move</h3><p>My friend, who is launching a new business selling gift baskets, proved the concept by selling them on Mother&#8217;s Day weekend. It was an experiment. She took a small step in the direction of her vision for the future. She started with an idea, but she found success because she made a move.</p><p>Successful people recognize that there is always a next move. When you do a deep dive into a subject like embracing change and making plans for your future, it can produce a lot of ideas. Ideas alone are not enough. You need to transform your ideas into actions. To help you move forward, the Asian Efficiency team has a resource that can assist you to <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/systems/how-to-turn-notes-into-actionable-steps/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Turn Notes Into Actionable Steps</a>.</p><h3>Next Steps</h3><p>Embrace change in your life and stay productive by</p><ol><li>Asking yourself clarifying questions</li><li>Identifying opportunities</li><li>Defining your next move</li></ol><p>Here&#8217;s how:</p><ul><li>Focus on just one big change that you are facing in your life.</li><li>In a text editor or on a piece of paper, write down your answers to the clarifying questions from this article.</li><li>Follow the process for identifying opportunities.</li><li>Take your first step. Don&#8217;t be afraid to take small steps. Even small changes can have a big impact.</li></ul><p>Are you currently working from home because of recent changes? <a href=\"https://asianefficiencygo.com/pah-webinar-registration/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Register here</a> to join our FREE training on how to be productive working from home. The training will include apps, tools, and routines that you can use.</p> ","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 14 Jul 2020 11:00:19 +0000","feedId":10782,"bgimg":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/pivot-to-success-portada.jpg","linkMd5":"7a2235c3303b71338f8f6c67bf2c8c6c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn18@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-05-12-401_d73ff7f605029c62.webp","destWidth":2500,"destHeight":1875,"sourceBytes":1245070,"destBytes":801658,"author":"Steve Robertson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/pivot-to-success-portada.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn18@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-05-12-401_d73ff7f605029c62.webp","https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/05/Johanna-Cestas-375x500.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn63@2020_4/2020/08/25/01-05-49-325_74790fc79bbc34b1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317508238},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","title":"Has The Pandemic Put You Behind On Your Goals? 09:05:08","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","title":"Should You Keep Or Drop Your Goal? Here’s How To Know If A Goal Is Worth Pursuing","link":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/?p=16199","description":"<p><img class=\"alignnone size-full wp-image-16200\" src=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/omnifocus-dropped-2.png\" alt=\"Drop Project in OmniFocus\" width=\"1189\" height=\"360\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/omnifocus-dropped-2.png 1189w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/omnifocus-dropped-2-500x151.png 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/omnifocus-dropped-2-768x233.png 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/omnifocus-dropped-2-640x194.png 640w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1189px) 100vw, 1189px\" /></p><p>Have you ever stared at a task on your to-do list and thought to yourself, “Is this really what I should be spending my time on?”</p><p>I went through this recently. I am a big believer in <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/podcast/240-personal-development-plan/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">ongoing education and development</a>, so I always have at least one course or tutorial on the go.</p><p>I had set a goal to complete a “full-stack developer” course that I had purchased on a Black Friday sale. I was enthusiastic about it, but there were a few problems:</p><ul><li>I haven’t done software development as a job since 2002.</li><li>Sometimes I do some coding here at AE, but it’s not (currently) using any of the technologies that were in the course.</li><li>It was an extremely long course, and I started getting disillusioned around the 21% mark.</li><li>I realized fairly quickly that the instructor and course materials weren’t great.</li></ul><p>On the flip side…</p><ul><li>I didn’t spend a ton of money on the course, but I did spend some. It would be a shame to “lose” it. (My college Management Accounting teachers would have a heart attack if they heard me say that.)</li><li>I am an insane completionist, and I have trouble stopping things once I start.</li><li>You never know, I might learn something that can improve how we do things at AE.</li><li><em>Nerd Alert</em> I do find the topic quite interesting, and it was a subject I wanted to learn more about.</li></ul><p>As an outsider looking in, it might seem obvious what I should do, but we humans are often horrible at this type of self-reflection, and we often don’t give this type of thought to our goals once we make them.</p><p>At the best of times, it can be hard to know what to prioritize. You may be feeling like you can’t get everything done in the time that you have — a lot of us do — so any time we spend on things that we shouldn’t compounds the problem.</p><p>Perhaps you’ve been in a similar situation. You’ve chosen (or been given) a goal, and you’re not sure if you are wasting your time or not. We understand, and we’ve helped hundreds of people in your situation.</p><p>So if you’re thinking of pursuing a goal, but you’re not sure if it is right for you, or if you’re partway through the year and have doubts if it is worth completing a goal, or if you always set goals and then flame out after a while, this article is for you. We’ll be sharing a framework you can use to figure out if a goal is still worth pursuing.</p><h2>It’s Not Only About Dropping A Goal</h2><p>So far, we’ve been talking about dropping a goal you’re not sure of, but that won’t always be the case. By doing some proactive evaluation, you’ll have one of two outcomes:</p><ol><li><strong>Yes, Let’s Go!</strong> You’ve looked at the goal, and even if you have some doubts, you’ve found that it is worth pursuing after all. That’s great! Now you know you should <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/podcast/209-focus-time/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">make sure you have the time to focus on it</a>. We have lots of <a href=\"https://shop.asianefficiency.com/dojo/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">resources in our Dojo community to help</a>.</li><li><strong>Drop It Like It’s Hot</strong> The goal seemed like a good idea at the time, but now — not so much. That’s OK! Now you have time to focus on other, more impactful things. Dropping a goal isn’t a failure; it’s a long-term win.</li></ol><h2>How Do You Figure Out if a Goal Is Worth Sticking With?</h2><p>With all that said, let’s take a look at how you can decide if your goal is something you should continue working towards</p><h3>1. Reflect: Do you have a strong WHY?</h3><p>Think about the goal you’re evaluating. Why did you want to do it in the first place?</p><p>Most of us skip spending time on this, which is a big mistake, but it’s a mistake you can undo now.</p><p>If you are honest about your “Why,” that will significantly inform your next steps. If you can’t come up with a good reason to do it, that’s a big red flag. There’s a very high chance that you should drop, or significantly change, the goal.</p><p>We talk about this more in our article <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/goals/goal-setting-and-getting-having-a-why/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Goal Setting and Goal Getting: Having a Why</a>.</p><p>There are two useful exercises you can use to drill down on your “Why”:</p><ol><li><strong>The Five Whys</strong> exercise. Often, the real reason we want to do something is not the first thing to come to mind. The Five Whys exercise is like peeling an onion. You ask “Why” five times, and go deeper and deeper until you get to the core reason. <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/motivation/why-willpower-doesnt-work/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">You can see an example of the Five Whys exercise here</a>.</li><li><strong>Thinking Time</strong>. Use specially set aside thinking time to ask yourself questions, dig into ideas, and get into the right mindset and mental framework. We talked about this in our <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/podcast/162-thinking-time/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Thinking Time podcast episode</a>, and we have a course about it in <a href=\"https://shop.asianefficiency.com/dojo/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">The Dojo</a>.</li></ol><p>By reviewing your goal against your true why you’ll a) have an idea whether you should proceed or not, and b) will be more likely to stick with it through to completion. Whenever things get bumpy, your why can keep you going.</p><p>Using my course as an example, here’s how things came out:</p><ul><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/274c.png\" alt=\"❌\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> The fact that I spent money on the course is not a good reason to keep going — <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/mindsets/sunk-costs/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">sunk cost fallacy</a>.</li><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/274c.png\" alt=\"❌\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> Completing something for the sake of completing it makes no sense.</li><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/2705.png\" alt=\"✅\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> Spending time learning something is better than watching Netflix or scrolling TikTok.</li><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/2705.png\" alt=\"✅\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> Skills I pick up may enable us to do more cool things at AE or might help me in my later career.</li><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/2705.png\" alt=\"✅\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> Learning something I enjoy but is not-directly-work-related is good for my brain and makes me happy.</li></ul><p>So far, I’d say we are edging on continuing with the goal, so we shall see what happens. I won’t drop it yet.</p><h3>2. Evaluate your current situation</h3><p>Whenever I’ve run into trouble with a goal, it is usually because the goal <em>sounded</em> great, and maybe the whys even checked out, but there was no realistic chance that the goal could have succeeded. If I were honest with my self or proactive, I would have realized this.</p><p>Look ahead. What are your:</p><ul><li><strong>Work commitments</strong>. Do you have big projects coming up that might impact your ability to achieve this goal?</li><li><strong>Family commitments</strong>. Do you have to take the kids to activities, help them with homework, care for elderly parents, or have a limited family time?</li><li><strong>Energy constraints</strong>. Are you wiped out after work or during the day? Do you have energetic time where you could truly focus on this goal?</li><li><strong>Financial constraints</strong>. Can you afford to achieve this goal, or would spending money on it cause you other problems?</li><li><strong>Economic factors</strong>. Is there anything going on (like, say, a recession or global pandemic) that might interfere with achieving your goal?</li></ul><p>If you think through your time, energy, and resources available, you should have a clearer idea of whether you can and should continue.</p><p>There’ll usually be one of three outcomes:</p><ol><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/1f645-200d-2640-fe0f.png\" alt=\"🙅‍♀️\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /><strong>Not going to happen</strong>: The situation just isn’t going to allow this goal to be successful. Better to pull the plug now.</li><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/1f57a.png\" alt=\"🕺\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> <strong>Looks good</strong>: You have the <a href=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/tea-framework/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">time, energy, and attention</a> resources to be successful with this goal. Carry on.</li><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/1f9d7-200d-2640-fe0f.png\" alt=\"🧗‍♀️\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> <strong>There’s a gap</strong>: There’s a gap between what you want to do and what you can do, but it should be possible to close that gap. What are you willing to sacrifice to achieve the goal?</li></ol><p>Evaluating my course goal:</p><ul><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/2705.png\" alt=\"✅\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> I can plug away at the course a little every day (or every few days). Even on days that I’m tired, doing a programming tutorial is not exactly working in a coal mine. If it is a priority for me, I can do it.</li></ul><h3>3. Break the goal down</h3><p>Often when our goals are “big,” they just seem unmanageable, and it is hard to start and keep progressing.</p><p>Usually, when that happens, it is a sign that the goal needs to be broken down or re-written.</p><p>We talk about this as one of our <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/goals/5-steps-setting-goals/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">5 Simple Steps to Setting Goals and Achieving Them</a> — do you know how to achieve what you want to achieve?</p><p>You may have created a goal at the start of the year or quarter, but didn’t adequately break down how you’re going to do it.</p><p>Breaking a goal down into smaller, more manageable pieces will help you <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/task-management/gtd-intro/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">determine your next actions</a>.</p><p>An important question about breaking down a goal: What if you don’t know everything you need to do to achieve your goal?</p><p>There’s a misconception that to break down a goal, you need to be able to <em>completely</em> break it down. If you can do that, that’s great! But sometimes it is not possible. Sometimes we don’t have the knowledge or clarity yet to know everything.</p><p>That is entirely normal. Even if you don’t know everything you need to do, there’s a good chance that you at least know the first two or three things. Start with that, and then you can continue when you get there.</p><p>Looking at my course goal:</p><ul><li><img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/26a0.png\" alt=\"⚠\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" /> Breaking down my development course, I realized that the problem wasn’t the Why or the Situation. The problem was that the specific course itself just wasn’t good. If I could shift the goal to have a similar outcome, but with better materials and a smaller time commitment, I could still keep the goal.</li></ul><h3>4. Start executing</h3><p>Making a goal is easy. Consistently executing on your goal and making progress? Not so easy.</p><p>The best way to beat the odds is to create daily rituals and systems to take you there.</p><p>Here are some resources to help:</p><ul><li><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/habits/why-habits-dont-work/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">5 Reasons Why Habits Don’t Work (And Why You Need Rituals Instead)</a></li><li><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/productivity/revisiting-the-12-week-year/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">The 12 Week Year: The “Secret Sauce” To Achieving Your Goals Every Quarter</a></li><li><a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/podcast/293-perfect-week/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">How To Plan Your Perfect Week In Under 45 Minutes</a></li></ul><p>If you want to kick-start your goal execution, <a href=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/announcements/dojo-2-0-turn-knowledge-into-action/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">check out our “Do The Damned Things” calls</a>. They’re all about turning knowledge into action and are laser-focused on execution and implementation.</p><p>You don’t need more productivity knowledge — you need productivity action!</p><h2>Next steps</h2><p>Here are the next steps we recommend to figure out whether one of your goals is worth pursuing:</p><ul><li>Pick one goal and come up with your WHY. Use the Five Whys exercise if necessary.</li><li>Look at your time, energy, financial, and attention circumstances &#8211; what could get in the way of you completing this goal?</li></ul><p>If you’re wondering — the outcome of my goal is I dropped the course I purchased and found another one. It is much better, and now I’m enjoying working towards my goal instead of dreading it. Similar goal, but more likely to succeed <img src=\"https://s.w.org/images/core/emoji/11/72x72/1f44d.png\" alt=\"👍\" class=\"wp-smiley\" style=\"height: 1em; max-height: 1em;\" />.</p> ","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 22 Jun 2020 11:00:31 +0000","feedId":10782,"bgimg":"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/TPS305-Header.jpg","linkMd5":"9c1b1782206fd9bf1cd3dadfd37dfb75","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn53@2020_4/2020/08/25/01-05-10-865_c8077fd1a0eaf9f3.webp","destWidth":980,"destHeight":350,"sourceBytes":180975,"destBytes":47266,"author":"Asian Efficiency Team","enclosureType":"audio/mpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://dts.podtrac.com/redirect.mp3/traffic.libsyn.com/secure/productivityshow/305_-_The_Productivity_Show_-_See_How_Easily_You_Can_Start_a_Side-Hustle_with_Jen_Shultz.mp3","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/06/TPS305-Header.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn53@2020_4/2020/08/25/01-05-10-865_c8077fd1a0eaf9f3.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/itunes-logo.150.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn72@2020_4/2020/08/25/01-05-49-191_3de616686d8dfb4b.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/sticher.150.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn23@2020_2/2020/08/25/01-05-50-320_80c57ad218498494.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/overcast_final1.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn23@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-05-49-489_c087859209696cac.webp","http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/pocket-cast.150.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn100@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-05-48-325_323e6c883aefdcda.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317508239},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:05:08","title":"How Hobbies Make You More Productive and Creative (and 7 Hobbies to Take Up Today)","link":"http://www.asianefficiency.com/?p=13606","description":"<p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13612\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-7-768x512.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"512\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-7-768x512.jpeg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-7-500x333.jpeg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-7-640x427.jpeg 640w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-7.jpeg 1280w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 768px) 100vw, 768px\" /></p><p>Like many people, you probably try to keep your work life and personal life separate — and the latter likely includes hobbies.</p><p>However, a growing body of research suggests the hobbies you engage in could boost your output at work. Let’s explore why that’s the case, then look at several hobbies you could try that may help you make the most of your efforts on the job.</p><h2>Your Hobbies Could Help You Learn New Skills</h2><p>Due to a fear of stepping too far outside of their comfort zones, many people stick to hobbies that are somewhat related to their jobs or things they already know they can do well.</p><p>However, a study from San Francisco State University involving nearly 350 employees found that when people participate in hobbies, they often make job-related improvements.</p><p>The scientists aren’t sure why that’s the case, but they suspect it’s because people <a href=\"http://time.com/65487/being-creative-outside-of-work-makes-you-better-at-your-job/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">learn new things while doing hobbies</a> that they can later apply to their work.</p><p>As a result, productivity goes up, and individuals become well-versed in capabilities that fit with the things they do while on the clock.</p><h2>They Force You to Manage Your Time Wisely</h2><p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13608\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-4-768x512.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"512\" /></p><p>Taking up a hobby usually requires rearranging your schedule to fit in the new activity. Plus, you have to stick to that plan or risk letting your work duties suffer or having to give up the pastime. If you need extra motivation to become a better manager of your time, a new hobby could provide the assistance you seek.</p><p>People typically feel proud of themselves for taking the initiative and learning something new, and they don’t want to become ashamed after finding out they can’t manage their time well enough to sustain the commitment the new activity needs. That’s especially true if a hobby involves a teacher or a team. When other people depend on you, you’re more likely to stay dedicated.</p><p>You can probably relate to how people are generally conscious of what others think of them. They could feel embarrassed by admitting to others that they can’t sufficiently work out their schedules to include both a hobby and work.</p><h2>Hobbies Help You Think About Obstacles in Different Ways</h2><p>Running into an intellectual roadblock that gives you the impression that’s no way to solve a certain problem can make your productivity plummet. However, an analysis suggests that having hobbies stimulates your mind to <a href=\"https://www.bakadesuyo.com/2012/07/are-hobbies-the-key-to-creative-breakthroughs/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">come up with innovative ideas</a>.</p><p>You might experience something while doing a hobby that gives you an alternative perspective on a task at work or makes you realize a difficult responsibility at work is not as hard as it seemed at first.</p><p>Also, you may want to prioritize hobbies that allow you to interact with others. In situations that permit giving and receiving feedback, you may confide in another person and admit you’re dealing with a problem at work that has you feeling stumped.</p><p>Sometimes the fresh perspective provided by another individual could be what’s needed to see you knew the answer all along but weren’t looking at the scenario with the proper perspective.</p><h2>They Increase Patience and Willingness to Work Toward Long-Term Goals</h2><p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13610\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-2-768x512.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"512\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-2-768x512.jpeg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-2-500x333.jpeg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-2-640x427.jpeg 640w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-2.jpeg 1280w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 768px) 100vw, 768px\" /></p><p>Although some aspects of productivity and creativity require short bursts of work, they also involve pouring your efforts into an objective that may take years to achieve. Think about your favorite composer or inventor. There’s a good chance the product of that person’s work ethic didn’t manifest overnight. It probably took months, and most likely, years.</p><p>Taking part in a hobby often involves learning smaller skills you can combine to get closer to a bigger goal. Think of playing the guitar as an example. Before learning advanced barre chords that require using at least one finger to hold down several or all of the strings at once, you’ll probably master significantly easier chords that might only need pressure put on two strings.</p><p>Regardless of the hobby chosen, that kind of progressive learning naturally teaches patience. When you have a more patient mindset about the things you set out to do, it could help you more easily see that it’s worthwhile to set your sights on productivity or creativity goals even if you don’t accomplish them for a while.</p><p>Patience is also closely associated with self-control, and scientists have found a link between greater levels of self-control <a href=\"https://www.psychologicalscience.org/observer/the-lifetime-effects-of-self-control-in-childhood\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">and having better health</a>. Furthermore, when people practiced self-control as kids, they were more likely to see its positive effects as adults.</p><p>Maybe self-control has never been one of your strong points, but it’s never too late to start developing it. You could see benefits such as not getting angry if things don’t go your way. It’s likely you’ll realize that not giving in to some of your unhealthy or counterproductive impulses while at work could help you get more done and improve the overall quality of your performance.</p><h2>Having Hobbies Could Make You Less Stressed</h2><p>When you feel upset due to stress, reduced productivity often follows. If that happens, you may begin engaging in a harsh internal dialogue with yourself that blames other factors besides the stress, thereby perpetuating a vicious cycle. However, a 2015 study found that people who <a href=\"https://www.ucmerced.edu/sites/ucmerced.edu/files/documents/zawadzki-paper-2015.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">spent time doing leisure activities</a> experienced less stress, had lower heart rates, and felt calmer than when they were not doing those things.</p><p>People often have good intentions of starting hobbies but never take the steps necessary to do so. If that sounds familiar, consider that your decision to take up a hobby could promote relaxation and stress relief, making you more able to excel while on the job.</p><h2>Hobbies Help You Recover From Demanding Days</h2><p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13609\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-1-768x512.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"512\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-1-768x512.jpeg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-1-500x333.jpeg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-1-640x427.jpeg 640w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-1.jpeg 1280w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 768px) 100vw, 768px\" /></p><p>Many people who strive for consistent productivity or creativity find it’s sometimes hard to stay on track because they don’t have sufficient recovery periods during their leisure time. That phenomenon frequently becomes more problematic when people blur the lines between work and pleasure by doing things such as taking career-related paperwork home with them at night to read outside of work hours.</p><p>However, a 2016 study that highlighted the recovery periods of people working at a hospital found that a cultural — and preferably creative — activity encourages recovery from taxing work and increases the likelihood of mastery.</p><p>Also, <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4939860/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">41 percent of the hospital workers</a> in the study had experienced moderate to poor recovery periods, and related research cited by the team found that those who do not learn effective methods of recovering are at higher risk for exhaustion and cynicism after one year.</p><p>When trying to get as much done as possible, people often don’t allow themselves to have as much downtime as needed for optimal performance. Even after reading some of the advantages of having hobbies, you may feel <a href=\"https://productivitytheory.com/should-you-give-up-hobbies-for-better-productivity-at-work/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">tempted to give up yours</a> due to the time management-related concerns mentioned earlier.</p><p>However, when you start sacrificing some non-work-related activities — such as hobbies — it becomes easier to justify other things you normally do in your downtime, like sleep.</p><p>Research carried out on mice found that slumber allowed the rodents to recalibrate neurons in their brains, helping to <a href=\"https://hub.jhu.edu/2017/02/02/sleep-brain-memories-mice-study/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">reinforce the lessons learned</a> during time away. That research strengthened the belief that the minds of living things can only hold so much information before requiring rest.</p><p>If you take care to set aside time for relaxation through hobbies, it should be easier to get enough shut-eye, too. Together, both those activities could help you excel while getting things done.</p><p>Now that you’ve gotten an overview of why hobbies help keep you creative and productive, let’s look at several of the best options to try.</p><h2>7 Hobbies to Foster Productivity and Creativity</h2><h3>1. Walking</h3><p>Many people believe that the ultimate way to stay as productive as possible at work is to stay in one place — usually their desks — and devote attention solely to the task at hand. However, if you’re stuck in a creative rut, research indicates it’s useful to break up your workday with a hobby that makes you move, such as walking</p><p>Charles Dickens and Virginia Woolf are two famous authors who kept up a regular walking habit for creativity reasons. Also, research from Stanford University showed that when people received mental tasks that required creativity, walking while doing them <a href=\"https://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/minding-the-body/201603/become-better-writer-be-frequent-walker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">led to more creativity</a> than sitting still.</p><p>Some people even take part in meditative walks and focus on things such as the wind blowing through their hair and the sound of their footsteps on the pavement. If you often feel it’s hard to remain productive due to the wild thoughts running through your head, meditating in that way could calm down the inner turmoil and make it easier for you to concentrate.</p><h3>2. Playing Video Games</h3><p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13607\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-3-768x512.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"512\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-3-768x512.jpeg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-3-500x333.jpeg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-3-640x427.jpeg 640w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-3.jpeg 1280w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 768px) 100vw, 768px\" /></p><p>You might think that video games are low on the mental stimulation scale compared to some activities, but that’s not always the case. A comprehensive review of the effect of video games on children found that role-playing and strategy-based games <a href=\"http://www.apa.org/monitor/2014/02/video-game.aspx\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">improved problem-solving abilities</a>.</p><p>One potential downside of a video game-playing hobby is that you might find it too solitary. In that case, consider looking for video game groups in your area that involve participants playing at the same time as at least one other person. The combined knowledge you already have about the game and the suggestions from other gaming fans could make it easier to progress through levels and tackle challenges compared to playing by yourself.</p><p>Also, consider how much more effectively you could work by solving problems quicker than usual, due in part to video games. In addition to helping you overcome challenges, improved problem-solving abilities could cause you to not find yourself in as many tricky situations that compromise an ability to work at your best.</p><h3>3. Learning a New Language</h3><p>If you only speak one language, substantial amounts of evidence indicate that learning another during your downtime could promote more alertness, improved multitasking, <a href=\"http://www.businessinsider.com/benefits-of-learning-another-language-2017-2\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">and other worthy benefits</a>. Also, it doesn’t necessarily take a long time to see those positive outcomes. In the case of the study that determined a link between language learning and alertness, those students were only enrolled in a one-week course.</p><p>To connect productivity with your language-learning goals, determine if there are things you typically do during a workday that could benefit from knowing another language. Do you live in an area that has a large Spanish-speaking population? Have representatives at your company recently signed a deal with a large company in France?</p><p>After learning a new language and becoming fluent, you could get more done by using your brain for language-related duties and being less reliant on Google Translate or a similar, potentially time-consuming service. If that outcome happens, you’d also be in a fantastic position to assert that you deserve a promotion.</p><h3>4. Stamp Collecting</h3><p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13613\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-5-768x512.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"512\" /></p><p>Stamp collecting is a pastime with a rich history. First established as a hobby in the late 1800s, it reached a peak in the 1970s when there were <a href=\"https://www.apfelbauminc.com/library/stamp-collecting-not-dead\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">over 1,000 prominent dealers</a> active in the United States. Even though it’s been around for generations, stamp collecting is a hobby that’s still worth pursuing.</p><p>Many people do so for investment reasons, knowing that some rare stamps can become extremely lucrative possessions. Regardless of if you’re in it for the money, collecting stamps can help you appreciate the creative motivations that went into producing specific limited-edition stamps. You could also engage with other stamp collectors in your state or city and meet with them for trading, sales, and discussions.</p><h3>5. Playing Chess</h3><p>Earlier, we explored the benefits of getting engrossed in video games as a hobby, but some other kinds of beneficial games aren’t as high tech. Chess is one of them, and research suggests it has <a href=\"https://mic.com/articles/119332/how-chess-players-brains-are-different-from-everybody-else-s#.x6mfMeW4A\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">several brain-building characteristics</a>. For example, it teaches you to think in patterns and use previous memories to make decisions, which could increase productivity.</p><p>Scientific evidence also shows that chess players have smaller brains compared to non-chess players. Although that may seem like a negative factor, the associated research indicated the smaller size could be a sign of increased neural efficiency. Furthermore, the act of playing chess makes people use both sides of their brains, which stimulates more advanced thought processes.</p><h3>6. Scrapbooking</h3><p>When going on a trip or reaching a milestone in life, you probably like to have some mementos to help you recall the occasion. To keep all their memories in one place, many people create scrapbooks. You may find that’s a fun and fruitful hobby, especially if you take pride in finishing projects and showing them to others.</p><p>Scrapbooking also lets you practice being both creative and productive. Creativity comes into the equation when you decide how to arrange photographs, ticket stubs, and other materials while simultaneously choosing which color of paper to use, whether to decorate the page with cutout shapes and if you should describe a page’s contents with a handwritten caption.</p><p>Productivity is a factor because if you take too long to complete each page of the scrapbook, you’ll likely lose interest before finishing the endeavor. You might even find it useful to set up a scrapbooking schedule. It’ll keep you on track by providing things to get involved in each day and help you keep tabs on the extent of the work you’re doing, thereby increasing self-esteem.</p><h3>7. Team Sports</h3><p><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium_large wp-image-13611\" src=\"http://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-6-768x511.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"768\" height=\"511\" srcset=\"https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-6-768x511.jpeg 768w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-6-500x332.jpeg 500w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-6-640x426.jpeg 640w, https://www.asianefficiency.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/03/hobbies-6.jpeg 1280w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 768px) 100vw, 768px\" /></p><p>A study carried out on workers in the United Kingdom found that sports boosts morale and <a href=\"https://www.spacesworks.com/sports-boosts-productivity-levels/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">make work environments more productive</a>. Perhaps that’s because people who get involved in sports can see their efforts pay off when they practice their disciplines. Although you’ll undoubtedly see some benefits by picking a sport you play independently, consider doing something that makes you part of a team.</p><p>Taking that approach could enhance your communication skills, making it easier to get results while on the playing field as well as at work. While interacting with their teammates, athletes often use body language, gestures, and similar techniques to tell fellow players things without giving the other team valuable strategies.</p><p>Also, recall the earlier discussion of the positive effect of hobbies on patience and self-control. Playing a sport with your team tends to increase those qualities. For example, you’ll have to show patience while learning new skills.</p><p>Self-control is also necessary when you have the option to practice with your teammates or binge-watch a favorite TV show. The latter may feel more tempting if you lack energy, but coming together with your teammates to work toward a common goal increases your focus and willpower.</p><p>If people at your workplace are up for the idea, think about asking your supervisor if you could organize a sports team made up of your colleagues. Then, you’d not only reap the benefits of having a hobby, but you’d also learn more about the people at your job and make your social life more diverse.</p><p>At first, hobbies may seem like productivity disruptors, but now, thanks to these studies and examples, you know better. By rearranging your schedule to make room for hobbies, you’ll likely notice some of the benefits described above, as well as others you’d hadn’t considered. As a result, you’ll be on a path to a better work-life balance, which could pay off in other ways besides more productivity and creativity.</p> ","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 28 Jul
